       Washington (disambiguation)
      </a>
      .

     <p>
      <b>
       Washington, D.C.
      </b>
      , formally the
      <b>
       District of Columbia
      </b>
      and commonly referred to as "
      <b>
       Washington
      </b>
      ", "
      <b>
       the District
      </b>
      ", or simply "
      <b>
       D.C.
      </b>
      ", is the
      <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">
       capital
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
       United States
      </a>
      . The signing of the
      <a href="/wiki/Residence_Act" title="Residence Act">
       Residence Act
      </a>
      on July 16, 1790, approved the creation of a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capital_district" title="Capital district">
       capital district
      </a>
      located along the
      <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">
       Potomac River
      </a>
      on the country's
      <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="East Coast of the United States">
       East Coast
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">
       U.S. Constitution
      </a>
      provided for a
      <a href="/wiki/Federal_district" title="Federal district">
       federal district
      </a>
      under the
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_home_rule" title="District of Columbia home rule">
       exclusive jurisdiction
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">
       Congress
      </a>
      and the District is therefore not a part of any state.
     </p>
     <p>
      The states of
      <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">
       Maryland
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">
       Virginia
      </a>
      each donated land to form the federal district, which included the pre-existing settlements of
      <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Georgetown (Washington, D.C.)">
       Georgetown
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia" title="Alexandria, Virginia">
       Alexandria
      </a>
      . Named in honor of President
      <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">
       George Washington
      </a>
      , the City of Washington was founded in 1791 to serve as the new national capital. In 1846, Congress
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_retrocession" title="District of Columbia retrocession">
       returned the land originally ceded by Virginia
      </a>
      ; in 1871, it created a
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_Organic_Act_of_1871" title="District of Columbia Organic Act of 1871">
       single municipal government
      </a>
      for the remaining portion of the District.
     </p>
     <p>
      Washington had an estimated population of 672,228 as of July 2015. Commuters from the surrounding Maryland and Virginia suburbs raise the city's population to more than one million during the workweek. The
      <a href="/wiki/Washington_metropolitan_area" title="Washington metropolitan area">
       Washington metropolitan area
      </a>
      , of which the District is a part, has a population of over 6 million, the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Table_of_United_States_Metropolitan_Statistical_Areas" title="Table of United States Metropolitan Statistical Areas">
       sixth-largest
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_statistical_area" title="Metropolitan statistical area">
       metropolitan statistical area
      </a>
      in the country.
     </p>
     <p>
      The centers of all three branches of the
      <a href="/wiki/Federal_government_of_the_United_States" title="Federal government of the United States">
       federal government of the United States
      </a>
      are in the District, including the Congress,
      <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_States" title="President of the United States">
       President
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">
       Supreme Court
      </a>
      . Washington is home to many national
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_museums_in_Washington,_D.C." title="List of museums in Washington, D.C.">
       monuments and museums
      </a>
      , which are primarily situated on or around the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Mall" title="National Mall">
       National Mall
      </a>
      . The city hosts 176
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_diplomatic_missions_in_the_United_States" title="List of diplomatic missions in the United States">
       foreign embassies
      </a>
      as well as the headquarters of many international organizations, trade unions, non-profit organizations, lobbying groups, and professional associations.
     </p>
     <p>
      A locally elected
      <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_the_District_of_Columbia" title="Mayor of the District of Columbia">
       mayor
      </a>
      and a 13‑member
      <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_District_of_Columbia" title="Council of the District of Columbia">
       council
      </a>
      have governed the District since 1973. However, the Congress maintains supreme authority over the city and may overturn local laws. D.C. residents elect a
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_voting_rights" title="District of Columbia voting rights">
       non-voting
      </a>
      , at-large
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-voting_members_of_the_United_States_House_of_representatives" title="Non-voting members of the United States House of representatives">
       congressional delegate
      </a>
      to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._House_of_Representatives" title="U.S. House of Representatives">
       House of Representatives
      </a>
      , but the District has no representation in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Senate" title="U.S. Senate">
       Senate
      </a>
      . The District receives three
      <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">
       electoral votes
      </a>
      in presidential elections as permitted by the
      <a href="/wiki/Twenty-third_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-third Amendment to the United States Constitution">
       Twenty-third Amendment to the United States Constitution
      </a>
      , ratified in 1961.
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="History">
       History
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/History_of_Washington,_D.C." title="History of Washington, D.C.">
       History of Washington, D.C.
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Washington,_D.C." title="Timeline of Washington, D.C.">
       Timeline of Washington, D.C.
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Various
      <a href="/wiki/Tribe" title="Tribe">
       tribes
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/Algonquian_peoples" title="Algonquian peoples">
       Algonquian
      </a>
      -speaking
      <a href="/wiki/Piscataway_tribe" title="Piscataway tribe">
       Piscataway people
      </a>
      (also known as the Conoy) inhabited the lands around the
      <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">
       Potomac River
      </a>
      when Europeans first visited the area in the early 17th century. One group known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Nacotchtank" title="Nacotchtank">
       Nacotchtank
      </a>
      (also called the Nacostines by
      <a href="/wiki/Catholic_missions" title="Catholic missions">
       Catholic missionaries
      </a>
      ) maintained settlements around the
      <a href="/wiki/Anacostia_River" title="Anacostia River">
       Anacostia River
      </a>
      within the present-day District of Columbia. Conflicts with
      <a href="/wiki/European_colonization_of_the_Americas" title="European colonization of the Americas">
       European colonists
      </a>
      and neighboring tribes forced the relocation of the Piscataway people, some of whom established a new settlement in 1699 near
      <a href="/wiki/Point_of_Rocks,_Maryland" title="Point of Rocks, Maryland">
       Point of Rocks, Maryland
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        [3]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In his
      <a href="/wiki/Federalist_No._43" title="Federalist No. 43">
       Federalist No. 43
      </a>
      , published January 23, 1788,
      <a href="/wiki/James_Madison" title="James Madison">
       James Madison
      </a>
      argued that the new federal government would need authority over a national capital to provide for its own maintenance and safety.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        [4]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Five years earlier, a band of unpaid soldiers besieged Congress while its members were meeting in
      <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia" title="Philadelphia">
       Philadelphia
      </a>
      . Known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Mutiny_of_1783" title="Pennsylvania Mutiny of 1783">
       Pennsylvania Mutiny of 1783
      </a>
      , the event emphasized the need for the national government not to rely on any state for its own security.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        [5]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Article_One_of_the_United_States_Constitution#Section_8:_Powers_of_Congress" title="Article One of the United States Constitution">
       Article One, Section Eight
      </a>
      , of the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">
       Constitution
      </a>
      permits the establishment of a "District (not exceeding ten miles square) as may, by cession of particular states, and the acceptance of Congress, become the seat of the government of the United States".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        [6]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the Constitution does not specify a location for the capital. In what is now known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1790" title="Compromise of 1790">
       Compromise of 1790
      </a>
      , Madison,
      <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton" title="Alexander Hamilton">
       Alexander Hamilton
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">
       Thomas Jefferson
      </a>
      came to an agreement that the federal government would pay each state's remaining Revolutionary War debts in exchange for establishing the new national capital in the
      <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">
       Southern United States
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crew124_7-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Crew124-7">
        [7]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        [a]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation">
       Foundation
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_District_of_Columbia,_1835.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3226" data-file-width="2451" height="224" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Map_of_the_District_of_Columbia%2C_1835.jpg/170px-Map_of_the_District_of_Columbia%2C_1835.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Map_of_the_District_of_Columbia%2C_1835.jpg/255px-Map_of_the_District_of_Columbia%2C_1835.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Map_of_the_District_of_Columbia%2C_1835.jpg/340px-Map_of_the_District_of_Columbia%2C_1835.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_District_of_Columbia,_1835.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Map of the District of Columbia in 1835, prior to the
        <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_retrocession" title="District of Columbia retrocession">
         retrocession
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On July 9, 1790, Congress passed the
      <a href="/wiki/Residence_Act" title="Residence Act">
       Residence Act
      </a>
      , which approved the creation of a national capital on the
      <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">
       Potomac River
      </a>
      . The exact location was to be selected by President
      <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">
       George Washington
      </a>
      , who signed the bill into law on July 16. Formed from land donated by the states of Maryland and Virginia, the initial shape of the federal district was a square measuring 10 miles (16 km) on each side, totaling 100 square miles (259 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crew89_9-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Crew89-9">
        [8]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        [b]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Two pre-existing settlements were included in the territory: the port of
      <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Georgetown (Washington, D.C.)">
       Georgetown, Maryland
      </a>
      , founded in 1751,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        [9]
       </a>
      </sup>
      and the city of
      <a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia" title="Alexandria, Virginia">
       Alexandria, Virginia
      </a>
      , founded in 1749.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        [10]
       </a>
      </sup>
      During 1791–92,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Andrew_Ellicott_(surveyor)" title="Andrew Ellicott (surveyor)">
       Andrew Ellicott
      </a>
      and several assistants, including a
      <a href="/wiki/Free_negro" title="Free negro">
       free African American
      </a>
      astronomer named
      <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Banneker" title="Benjamin Banneker">
       Benjamin Banneker
      </a>
      , surveyed the borders of the federal district and placed
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Boundary_Stones_(District_of_Columbia)" title="Boundary Stones (District of Columbia)">
       boundary stones
      </a>
      at every mile point.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        [11]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many of the stones are still standing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        [12]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A new
      <a href="/wiki/Federal_city" title="Federal city">
       federal city
      </a>
      was then constructed on the north bank of the Potomac, to the east of Georgetown. On September 9, 1791, the three commissioners overseeing the capital's construction named the city in honor of President Washington. The federal district was named
      <a href="/wiki/Columbia_(name)" title="Columbia (name)">
       Columbia
      </a>
      , which was a poetic name for the United States commonly in use at that time.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
       <a href="#cite_note-15">
        [13]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        [14]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Congress held its first session in Washington on November 17, 1800.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        [15]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Congress passed the
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_Organic_Act_of_1801" title="District of Columbia Organic Act of 1801">
       Organic Act of 1801
      </a>
      , which officially organized the District and placed the entire territory under the
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_home_rule" title="District of Columbia home rule">
       exclusive control
      </a>
      of the federal government. Further, the unincorporated area within the District was organized into two counties: the
      <a href="/wiki/Washington_County,_D.C." title="Washington County, D.C.">
       County of Washington
      </a>
      to the east of the Potomac and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alexandria_County,_D.C." title="Alexandria County, D.C.">
       County of Alexandria
      </a>
      to the west.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        [16]
       </a>
      </sup>
      After the passage of this Act, citizens living in the District were no longer considered residents of Maryland or Virginia, which therefore ended their representation in Congress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
       <a href="#cite_note-19">
        [17]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fords_Theatre.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="330" data-file-width="530" height="137" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Fords_Theatre.jpg/220px-Fords_Theatre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Fords_Theatre.jpg/330px-Fords_Theatre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Fords_Theatre.jpg/440px-Fords_Theatre.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fords_Theatre.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Ford%27s_Theatre" title="Ford's Theatre">
         Ford's Theatre
        </a>
        in the 19th century, site of the 1865
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln_assassination" title="Abraham Lincoln assassination">
         assassination
        </a>
        of
        <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">
         President Lincoln
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      On August 24–25, 1814, in a raid known as the
      <a href="/wiki/Burning_of_Washington" title="Burning of Washington">
       Burning of Washington
      </a>
      , British forces invaded the capital during the
      <a href="/wiki/War_of_1812" title="War of 1812">
       War of 1812
      </a>
      . The
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">
       Capitol
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_the_Treasury" title="United States Department of the Treasury">
       Treasury
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">
       White House
      </a>
      were burned and gutted during the attack.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        [18]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Most government buildings were repaired quickly; however, the Capitol was largely under construction at the time and was not completed in its current form until 1868.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        [19]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Retrocession_and_the_Civil_War">
       Retrocession and the Civil War
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_retrocession" title="District of Columbia retrocession">
       District of Columbia retrocession
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C.,_in_the_American_Civil_War" title="Washington, D.C., in the American Civil War">
       Washington, D.C., in the American Civil War
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      In the 1830s, the District's southern territory of Alexandria went into economic decline partly due to neglect by Congress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-richards_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-richards-22">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The city of Alexandria was a major market in the American
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Slave_trade" title="Slave trade">
       slave trade
      </a>
      , and pro-slavery residents feared that
      <a href="/wiki/Abolitionism_in_the_United_States" title="Abolitionism in the United States">
       abolitionists
      </a>
      in Congress would end
      <a href="/wiki/Slavery_in_the_United_States" title="Slavery in the United States">
       slavery
      </a>
      in the District, further depressing the economy. Alexandria's citizens petitioned Virginia to take back the land it had donated to form the District, through a process known as
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_retrocession" title="District of Columbia retrocession">
       retrocession
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        [21]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Virginia_General_Assembly" title="Virginia General Assembly">
       Virginia General Assembly
      </a>
      voted in February 1846 to accept the return of Alexandria and on July 9, 1846, Congress agreed to return all the territory that had been ceded by Virginia. Therefore, the District's current area consists only of the portion originally donated by Maryland.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-richards_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-richards-22">
        [20]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Confirming the fears of pro-slavery Alexandrians, the
      <a href="/wiki/Compromise_of_1850" title="Compromise of 1850">
       Compromise of 1850
      </a>
      outlawed the slave trade in the District, although not slavery itself.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        [22]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The outbreak of the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">
       American Civil War
      </a>
      in 1861 led to expansion of the federal government and notable growth in the District's population, including a large influx of freed slaves.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodd_25-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dodd-25">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
      President
      <a href="/wiki/Abraham_Lincoln" title="Abraham Lincoln">
       Abraham Lincoln
      </a>
      signed the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Compensated_Emancipation_Act" title="Compensated Emancipation Act">
       Compensated Emancipation Act
      </a>
      in 1862, which ended slavery in the District of Columbia and freed about 3,100 enslaved persons, nine months prior to the
      <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Proclamation" title="Emancipation Proclamation">
       Emancipation Proclamation
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emancipation_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-emancipation-26">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1868, Congress granted the District's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/African_American" title="African American">
       African American
      </a>
      male residents the right to vote in municipal elections.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dodd_25-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dodd-25">
        [23]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Growth_and_redevelopment">
       Growth and redevelopment
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:March_on_Washington_edit.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1902" data-file-width="1271" height="254" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/March_on_Washington_edit.jpg/170px-March_on_Washington_edit.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/March_on_Washington_edit.jpg/255px-March_on_Washington_edit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/March_on_Washington_edit.jpg/340px-March_on_Washington_edit.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:March_on_Washington_edit.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Crowds surrounding the
        <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Memorial_Reflecting_Pool" title="Lincoln Memorial Reflecting Pool">
         Reflecting Pool
        </a>
        during the 1963
        <a href="/wiki/March_on_Washington_for_Jobs_and_Freedom" title="March on Washington for Jobs and Freedom">
         March on Washington
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      By 1870, the District's population had grown 75% from the previous census to nearly 132,000 residents.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-histpop_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-histpop-27">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite the city's growth, Washington still had dirt roads and lacked basic sanitation. Some members of Congress suggested moving the capital further west, but President
      <a href="/wiki/Ulysses_S._Grant" title="Ulysses S. Grant">
       Ulysses S. Grant
      </a>
      refused to consider such a proposal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        [26]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Congress passed the
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_Organic_Act_of_1871" title="District of Columbia Organic Act of 1871">
       Organic Act of 1871
      </a>
      , which repealed the individual charters of the cities of Washington and Georgetown, and created a new territorial government for the whole District of Columbia.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        [27]
       </a>
      </sup>
      President Grant appointed
      <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Robey_Shepherd" title="Alexander Robey Shepherd">
       Alexander Robey Shepherd
      </a>
      to the position of governor in 1873. Shepherd authorized large-scale projects that greatly modernized Washington, but ultimately bankrupted the District government. In 1874, Congress replaced the territorial government with an appointed three-member Board of Commissioners.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        [28]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The city's
      <a href="/wiki/Streetcars_in_Washington,_D.C." title="Streetcars in Washington, D.C.">
       first motorized streetcars
      </a>
      began service in 1888 and generated growth in areas of the District beyond the City of Washington's original boundaries. Washington's urban plan was expanded throughout the District in the following decades.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neighborhoods_31-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-neighborhoods-31">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Georgetown was formally annexed by the City of Washington in 1895.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tindall_32-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tindall-32">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the city had poor housing conditions and strained public works. Washington was the first city in the nation to undergo
      <a href="/wiki/Urban_renewal" title="Urban renewal">
       urban renewal
      </a>
      projects as part of the "
      <a href="/wiki/City_Beautiful_movement" title="City Beautiful movement">
       City Beautiful movement
      </a>
      " in the early 1900s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ramroth_33-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ramroth-33">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Increased federal spending as a result of the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Deal" title="New Deal">
       New Deal
      </a>
      in the 1930s led to the construction of new government buildings, memorials, and museums in Washington.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        [32]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">
       World War II
      </a>
      further increased government activity, adding to the number of federal employees in the capital;
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        [33]
       </a>
      </sup>
      by 1950, the District's population reached its peak of 802,178 residents.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-histpop_27-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-histpop-27">
        [25]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights_and_home_rule_era">
       Civil rights and home rule era
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Twenty-third_Amendment_to_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Twenty-third Amendment to the United States Constitution">
       Twenty-third Amendment to the United States Constitution
      </a>
      was ratified in 1961, granting the District three votes in the
      <a href="/wiki/Electoral_College_(United_States)" title="Electoral College (United States)">
       Electoral College
      </a>
      for the election of president and vice president, but still no voting representation in Congress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
       <a href="#cite_note-36">
        [34]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      After the
      <a href="/wiki/Assassination_of_Martin_Luther_King_Jr." title="Assassination of Martin Luther King Jr.">
       assassination of civil rights leader Dr. Martin Luther King, Jr.
      </a>
      , on April 4, 1968,
      <a href="/wiki/1968_Washington,_D.C._riots" title="1968 Washington, D.C. riots">
       riots broke out in the District
      </a>
      , primarily in the U Street, 14th Street, 7th Street, and H Street corridors, centers of black residential and commercial areas. The riots raged for three days until more than 13,600 federal troops stopped the violence. Many stores and other buildings were burned; rebuilding was not completed until the late 1990s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37">
       <a href="#cite_note-37">
        [35]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1973, Congress enacted the
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_Home_Rule_Act" title="District of Columbia Home Rule Act">
       District of Columbia Home Rule Act
      </a>
      , providing for an elected mayor and 13-member council for the District.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
       <a href="#cite_note-38">
        [36]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1975,
      <a href="/wiki/Walter_Washington" title="Walter Washington">
       Walter Washington
      </a>
      became the first elected and first black mayor of the District.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        [37]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      On
      <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">
       September 11, 2001
      </a>
      , terrorists hijacked
      <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines_Flight_77" title="American Airlines Flight 77">
       American Airlines Flight 77
      </a>
      and deliberately crashed the plane into
      <a href="/wiki/The_Pentagon" title="The Pentagon">
       the Pentagon
      </a>
      in nearby
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arlington,_Virginia" title="Arlington, Virginia">
       Arlington, Virginia
      </a>
      .
      <a href="/wiki/United_Airlines_Flight_93" title="United Airlines Flight 93">
       United Airlines Flight 93
      </a>
      , believed to be destined for Washington, D.C., crashed in
      <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">
       Pennsylvania
      </a>
      when passengers tried to recover control of the plane from hijackers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        [38]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
       <a href="#cite_note-41">
        [39]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">
       Geography
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_Washington,_D.C." title="Geography of Washington, D.C.">
       Geography of Washington, D.C.
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:C%26O_Canal_-_Georgetown.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2581" data-file-width="2218" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/C%26O_Canal_-_Georgetown.jpg/170px-C%26O_Canal_-_Georgetown.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/C%26O_Canal_-_Georgetown.jpg/255px-C%26O_Canal_-_Georgetown.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/C%26O_Canal_-_Georgetown.jpg/340px-C%26O_Canal_-_Georgetown.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:C%26O_Canal_-_Georgetown.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_and_Ohio_Canal" title="Chesapeake and Ohio Canal">
         Chesapeake and Ohio Canal
        </a>
        passes through the
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgetown,_Washington,_D.C." title="Georgetown, Washington, D.C.">
         Georgetown
        </a>
        neighborhood.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Washington, D.C., is located in the
      <a href="/wiki/Mid-Atlantic_states" title="Mid-Atlantic states">
       mid-Atlantic region
      </a>
      of the
      <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="East Coast of the United States">
       U.S. East Coast
      </a>
      . Due to the
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_retrocession" title="District of Columbia retrocession">
       District of Columbia retrocession
      </a>
      , the city has a total area of 68.34 square miles (177.0 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ), of which 61.05 square miles (158.1 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) is land and 7.29 square miles (18.9 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) (10.67%) is water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        [40]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The District is bordered by
      <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_County,_Maryland" title="Montgomery County, Maryland">
       Montgomery County, Maryland
      </a>
      , to the northwest;
      <a href="/wiki/Prince_George%27s_County,_Maryland" title="Prince George's County, Maryland">
       Prince George's County, Maryland
      </a>
      , to the east; and
      <a href="/wiki/Arlington_County,_Virginia" title="Arlington County, Virginia">
       Arlington
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia" title="Alexandria, Virginia">
       Alexandria, Virginia
      </a>
      , to the south and west.
     </p>
     <p>
      The south bank of the
      <a href="/wiki/Potomac_River" title="Potomac River">
       Potomac River
      </a>
      forms the District's border with Virginia and has two major tributaries: the
      <a href="/wiki/Anacostia_River" title="Anacostia River">
       Anacostia River
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Rock_Creek_(Potomac_River)" title="Rock Creek (Potomac River)">
       Rock Creek
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        [41]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Tiber_Creek" title="Tiber Creek">
       Tiber Creek
      </a>
      , a natural watercourse that once passed through the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Mall" title="National Mall">
       National Mall
      </a>
      , was fully enclosed underground during the 1870s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        [42]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The creek also formed a portion of the now-filled
      <a href="/wiki/Washington_City_Canal" title="Washington City Canal">
       Washington City Canal
      </a>
      , which allowed passage through the city to the Anacostia River from 1815 until the 1850s.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        [43]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_and_Ohio_Canal" title="Chesapeake and Ohio Canal">
       Chesapeake and Ohio Canal
      </a>
      starts in Georgetown and was used during the 19th century to bypass the
      <a href="/wiki/Little_Falls_(Potomac_River)" title="Little Falls (Potomac River)">
       Little Falls
      </a>
      of the Potomac River, located at the northwest edge of Washington at the
      <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Seaboard_fall_line" title="Atlantic Seaboard fall line">
       Atlantic Seaboard fall line
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        [44]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The highest natural elevation in the District is 409 feet (125 m)
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Above_mean_sea_level" title="Above mean sea level">
       above sea level
      </a>
      at
      <a href="/wiki/Fort_Reno_Park" title="Fort Reno Park">
       Fort Reno Park
      </a>
      in upper northwest Washington.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        [45]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The lowest point is sea level at the Potomac River.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        [46]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The geographic center of Washington is near the intersection of 4th and L Streets NW.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        [47]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Contrary to the urban legend, Washington was not built on a reclaimed swamp, but wetlands did cover areas along the water.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        [48]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-legend_51-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-legend-51">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The District has 7,464 acres (30.21 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) of parkland, about 19% of the city's total area and the second-highest percentage among high-density U.S. cities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52">
       <a href="#cite_note-52">
        [50]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/National_Park_Service" title="National Park Service">
       National Park Service
      </a>
      manages most of the 9,122 acres (36.92 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) of city land owned by the U.S. government.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53">
       <a href="#cite_note-53">
        [51]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Rock_Creek_Park" title="Rock Creek Park">
       Rock Creek Park
      </a>
      is a 1,754-acre (7.10 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) urban forest in Northwest Washington, which extends 9.3 miles (15.0 km) through a stream valley that bisects the city. Established in 1890, it is the country's fourth-oldest national park and is home to a variety of plant and animal species including raccoon, deer, owls, and coyotes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        [52]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other National Park Service properties include the
      <a href="/wiki/Chesapeake_and_Ohio_Canal_National_Historical_Park" title="Chesapeake and Ohio Canal National Historical Park">
       C&amp;O Canal National Historical Park
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Mall_and_Memorial_Parks" title="National Mall and Memorial Parks">
       National Mall and Memorial Parks
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Theodore_Roosevelt_Island" title="Theodore Roosevelt Island">
       Theodore Roosevelt Island
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Island_(District_of_Columbia)" title="Columbia Island (District of Columbia)">
       Columbia Island
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Fort_Dupont_Park" title="Fort Dupont Park">
       Fort Dupont Park
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Meridian_Hill_Park" title="Meridian Hill Park">
       Meridian Hill Park
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Kenilworth_Park_and_Aquatic_Gardens" title="Kenilworth Park and Aquatic Gardens">
       Kenilworth Park and Aquatic Gardens
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Anacostia_Park" title="Anacostia Park">
       Anacostia Park
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        [53]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D.C._Department_of_Parks_and_Recreation" title="D.C. Department of Parks and Recreation">
       D.C. Department of Parks and Recreation
      </a>
      maintains the city's 900 acres (3.6 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ) of athletic fields and playgrounds, 40 swimming pools, and 68 recreation centers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        [54]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Agriculture" title="U.S. Department of Agriculture">
       U.S. Department of Agriculture
      </a>
      operates the 446-acre (1.80 km
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      )
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._National_Arboretum" title="U.S. National Arboretum">
       U.S. National Arboretum
      </a>
      in Northeast Washington.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        [55]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">
       Climate
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Maryland_hurricanes_(1950%E2%80%93present)" title="List of Maryland hurricanes (1950–present)">
       List of Maryland hurricanes (1950–present)
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Washington,_D.C._tornadoes" title="List of Washington, D.C. tornadoes">
       List of Washington, D.C. tornadoes
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cherry_Blossoms_and_Washington_Monument.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1479" data-file-width="2283" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Cherry_Blossoms_and_Washington_Monument.jpg/220px-Cherry_Blossoms_and_Washington_Monument.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Cherry_Blossoms_and_Washington_Monument.jpg/330px-Cherry_Blossoms_and_Washington_Monument.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Cherry_Blossoms_and_Washington_Monument.jpg/440px-Cherry_Blossoms_and_Washington_Monument.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cherry_Blossoms_and_Washington_Monument.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/National_Cherry_Blossom_Festival" title="National Cherry Blossom Festival">
         National Cherry Blossom Festival
        </a>
        is celebrated around the city each spring.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Washington is in the northern part of the
      <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">
       humid subtropical climate
      </a>
      zone (
      <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">
       Köppen
      </a>
      :
      <i>
       Cfa
      </i>
      )
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        [56]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, under the
      <a href="/wiki/Trewartha_climate_classification" title="Trewartha climate classification">
       Trewartha climate classification
      </a>
      , the city has a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">
       temperate
      </a>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maritime_climate" title="Maritime climate">
       maritime climate
      </a>
      (
      <i>
       Do
      </i>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        [57]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Winters are usually chilly with light snow, and summers are hot and humid. The District is in plant
      <a href="/wiki/Hardiness_zone" title="Hardiness zone">
       hardiness zone
      </a>
      8a near downtown, and zone 7b elsewhere in the city, indicating a humid subtropical climate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        [58]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Spring and fall are mild to warm, while winter is chilly with annual snowfall averaging 15.5 inches (39 cm). Winter temperatures average around 38 °F (3.3 °C) from mid-December to mid-February.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_Baltimore.2FWashington_.28LWX.29_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-NWS_Baltimore.2FWashington_.28LWX.29-61">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Summers are hot and humid with a July daily average of 79.8 °F (26.6 °C) and average daily relative humidity around 66%, which can cause moderate personal discomfort.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62">
       <a href="#cite_note-62">
        [60]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The combination of heat and humidity in the summer brings very frequent thunderstorms, some of which occasionally produce tornadoes in the area.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        [61]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Blizzards affect Washington on average once every four to six years. The most violent storms are called "
      <a href="/wiki/Nor%27easter" title="Nor'easter">
       nor'easters
      </a>
      ", which often affect large sections of the East Coast.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dcwinter_64-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-dcwinter-64">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      From
      <a href="/wiki/Knickerbocker_storm" title="Knickerbocker storm">
       January 27 to January 28, 1922
      </a>
      , the city officially received 28 inches (71 cm) of snowfall, the largest snowstorm since official measurements began in 1885.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        [63]
       </a>
      </sup>
      According to notes kept at the time, the city received between 30 and 36 inches (76 and 91 cm) from a snowstorm on January 1772.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DOC_66-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DOC-66">
        [64]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Hurricanes (or their remnants) occasionally track through the area in late summer and early fall, but are often weak by the time they reach Washington, partly due to the city's inland location.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        [65]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Flooding of the Potomac River, however, caused by a combination of high tide, storm surge, and runoff, has been known to cause extensive property damage in the neighborhood of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Georgetown,_Washington,_D.C." title="Georgetown, Washington, D.C.">
       Georgetown
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        [66]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Precipitation occurs throughout the year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMO_1961.E2.80.9390_KDCA_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-WMO_1961.E2.80.9390_KDCA-69">
        [67]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The highest recorded temperature was 106 °F (41 °C) on August 6, 1918, and on July 20, 1930.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Samenow_70-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Samenow-70">
        [68]
       </a>
      </sup>
      while the lowest recorded temperature was −15 °F (−26 °C) on February 11, 1899, during the
      <a href="/wiki/Great_Blizzard_of_1899" title="Great Blizzard of 1899">
       Great Blizzard of 1899
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dcwinter_64-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-dcwinter-64">
        [62]
       </a>
      </sup>
      During a typical year, the city averages about 37 days at or above 90 °F (32.2 °C) and 64 nights at or below freezing.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NWS_Baltimore.2FWashington_.28LWX.29_61-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-NWS_Baltimore.2FWashington_.28LWX.29-61">
        [59]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>
     <p>
      <br/>
     </p>

      <tr style="text-align: center;">

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Cityscape">
       Cityscape
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Streets_and_highways_of_Washington,_D.C." title="Streets and highways of Washington, D.C.">
       Streets and highways of Washington, D.C.
      </a>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Neighborhoods_in_Washington,_D.C." title="Neighborhoods in Washington, D.C.">
       Neighborhoods in Washington, D.C.
      </a>
      ; and
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_Washington,_D.C." title="List of tallest buildings in Washington, D.C.">
       List of tallest buildings in Washington, D.C.
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:L%27enfant_plan_of_Washington_DC.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="279" data-file-width="450" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/L%27enfant_plan_of_Washington_DC.jpg/220px-L%27enfant_plan_of_Washington_DC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/L%27enfant_plan_of_Washington_DC.jpg/330px-L%27enfant_plan_of_Washington_DC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/L%27enfant_plan_of_Washington_DC.jpg/440px-L%27enfant_plan_of_Washington_DC.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:L%27enfant_plan_of_Washington_DC.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        L'Enfant Plan for the federal capital city
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Washington, D.C., is a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_town" title="New town">
       planned city
      </a>
      . In 1791, President Washington commissioned
      <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Charles_L%27Enfant" title="Pierre Charles L'Enfant">
       Pierre (Peter) Charles L'Enfant
      </a>
      , a French-born architect and city planner, to design the new capital. The
      <a href="/wiki/L%27Enfant_Plan" title="L'Enfant Plan">
       L'Enfant Plan
      </a>
      featured broad streets and avenues radiating out from rectangles, providing room for open space and landscaping.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lenfant_76-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lenfant-76">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      He based his design on plans of cities such as
      <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">
       Paris
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">
       Amsterdam
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Karlsruhe" title="Karlsruhe">
       Karlsruhe
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">
       Milan
      </a>
      that
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson" title="Thomas Jefferson">
       Thomas Jefferson
      </a>
      had sent to him.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        [73]
       </a>
      </sup>
      L'Enfant's design also envisioned a garden-lined "grand avenue" approximately 1 mile (1.6 km) in length and 400 feet (120 m) wide in the area that is now the National Mall.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        [74]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:L%27Enfant_plan.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3404" data-file-width="4179" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/L%27Enfant_plan.jpg/220px-L%27Enfant_plan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/L%27Enfant_plan.jpg/330px-L%27Enfant_plan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/L%27Enfant_plan.jpg/440px-L%27Enfant_plan.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:L%27Enfant_plan.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/L%27Enfant_Plan" title="L'Enfant Plan">
         L'Enfant Plan
        </a>
        for Washington, D.C., as revised by
        <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Ellicott" title="Andrew Ellicott">
         Andrew Ellicott
        </a>
        in 1792
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      President Washington dismissed L'Enfant in March 1792 due to conflicts with the three commissioners appointed to supervise the capital's construction.
      <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Ellicott" title="Andrew Ellicott">
       Andrew Ellicott
      </a>
      , who had worked with L'Enfant surveying the city, was then tasked with completing the design. Though Ellicott made revisions to the original plans, including changes to some street patterns, L'Enfant is still credited with the overall design of the city.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        [75]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      By the early 1900s, L'Enfant's vision of a grand national capital had become marred by slums and randomly placed buildings, including a railroad station on the National Mall. Congress formed a special committee charged with beautifying Washington's ceremonial core.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ramroth_33-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ramroth-33">
        [31]
       </a>
      </sup>
      What became known as the
      <a href="/wiki/McMillan_Plan" title="McMillan Plan">
       McMillan Plan
      </a>
      was finalized in 1901 and included re-landscaping the Capitol grounds and the National Mall, clearing slums, and establishing a new citywide park system. The plan is thought to have largely preserved L'Enfant's intended design.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lenfant_76-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-lenfant-76">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Cairo_Building_in_Washington_DC.jpg">
        <img alt="A tall red brick building in the center of a city skyline punctuated by steeples and other shorter buildings." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="2048" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/The_Cairo_Building_in_Washington_DC.jpg/220px-The_Cairo_Building_in_Washington_DC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/The_Cairo_Building_in_Washington_DC.jpg/330px-The_Cairo_Building_in_Washington_DC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/The_Cairo_Building_in_Washington_DC.jpg/440px-The_Cairo_Building_in_Washington_DC.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Cairo_Building_in_Washington_DC.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The construction of the 12-story
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cairo_Apartment_Building" title="Cairo Apartment Building">
         Cairo Apartment Building
        </a>
        in 1894 spurred the city's
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Height_of_Buildings_Act_of_1899" title="The Height of Buildings Act of 1899">
         first building height restrictions
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      By law, Washington's skyline is low and sprawling. The federal
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heights_of_Buildings_Act_of_1910" title="Heights of Buildings Act of 1910">
       Heights of Buildings Act of 1910
      </a>
      allows buildings that are no taller than the width of the adjacent street, plus 20 feet (6.1 m).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartzman_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schwartzman-80">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite popular belief, no law has ever limited buildings to the height of the United States Capitol or the 555-foot (169 m)
      <a href="/wiki/Washington_Monument" title="Washington Monument">
       Washington Monument
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-legend_51-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-legend-51">
        [49]
       </a>
      </sup>
      which remains the District's tallest structure. City leaders have criticized the height restriction as a primary reason why the District has limited affordable housing and traffic problems caused by urban sprawl.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Schwartzman_80-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Schwartzman-80">
        [76]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The District is divided into
      <a href="/wiki/Quadrants_of_Washington,_D.C." title="Quadrants of Washington, D.C.">
       four quadrants
      </a>
      of unequal area:
      <a href="/wiki/Northwest,_Washington,_D.C." title="Northwest, Washington, D.C.">
       Northwest (NW)
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Northeast,_Washington,_D.C." title="Northeast, Washington, D.C.">
       Northeast (NE)
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Southeast,_Washington,_D.C." title="Southeast, Washington, D.C.">
       Southeast (SE)
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Southwest,_Washington,_D.C." title="Southwest, Washington, D.C.">
       Southwest (SW)
      </a>
      . The axes bounding the quadrants radiate from the U.S. Capitol building.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dcstreets_81-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-dcstreets-81">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
      All road names include the quadrant abbreviation to indicate their location and house numbers generally correspond with the number of blocks away from the Capitol. Most streets are set out in a grid pattern with east–west streets named with letters (
      <i>
       e.g.
      </i>
      , C Street SW), north–south streets with numbers (
      <i>
       e.g.
      </i>
      , 4th Street NW), and diagonal avenues, many of which are
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_state-named_roadways_in_Washington,_D.C." title="List of state-named roadways in Washington, D.C.">
       named after states
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dcstreets_81-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-dcstreets-81">
        [77]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The City of Washington was bordered by Boundary Street to the north (renamed
      <a href="/wiki/Florida_Avenue" title="Florida Avenue">
       Florida Avenue
      </a>
      in 1890), Rock Creek to the west, and the Anacostia River to the east.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-neighborhoods_31-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-neighborhoods-31">
        [29]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lenfant_76-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-lenfant-76">
        [72]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Washington's street grid was extended, where possible, throughout the District starting in 1888.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        [78]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_street_renaming" title="Georgetown street renaming">
       Georgetown's streets
      </a>
      were renamed in 1895.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tindall_32-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Tindall-32">
        [30]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some streets are particularly noteworthy, such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Avenue_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Pennsylvania Avenue (Washington, D.C.)">
       Pennsylvania Avenue
      </a>
      , which connects the White House to the Capitol and
      <a href="/wiki/K_Street_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="K Street (Washington, D.C.)">
       K Street
      </a>
      , which houses the offices of many lobbying groups.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kstreet_83-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-kstreet-83">
        [79]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Washington hosts 297
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_diplomatic_missions_in_Washington,_D.C." title="List of diplomatic missions in Washington, D.C.">
       foreign embassies
      </a>
      and related buildings, many of which are on a section of
      <a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Avenue_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Massachusetts Avenue (Washington, D.C.)">
       Massachusetts Avenue
      </a>
      informally known as
      <a href="/wiki/Embassy_Row" title="Embassy Row">
       Embassy Row
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-embassies_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-embassies-84">
        [80]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">
       Architecture
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1196" data-file-width="1614" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG/220px-WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG/330px-WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG/440px-WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:WhiteHouseSouthFacade.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">
         White House
        </a>
        ranked second on the
        <a href="/wiki/American_Institute_of_Architects" title="American Institute of Architects">
         AIA's
        </a>
        "
        <a href="/wiki/America%27s_Favorite_Architecture" title="America's Favorite Architecture">
         List of America's Favorite Architecture
        </a>
        " in 2007.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The architecture of Washington varies greatly. Six of the top 10 buildings in the
      <a href="/wiki/American_Institute_of_Architects" title="American Institute of Architects">
       American Institute of Architects
      </a>
      ' 2007 ranking of "
      <a href="/wiki/America%27s_Favorite_Architecture" title="America's Favorite Architecture">
       America's Favorite Architecture
      </a>
      " are in the District of Columbia:
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        [81]
       </a>
      </sup>
      the
      <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">
       White House
      </a>
      ; the
      <a href="/wiki/Washington_National_Cathedral" title="Washington National Cathedral">
       Washington National Cathedral
      </a>
      ; the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thomas_Jefferson_Memorial" title="Thomas Jefferson Memorial">
       Thomas Jefferson Memorial
      </a>
      ; the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">
       United States Capitol
      </a>
      ; the
      <a href="/wiki/Lincoln_Memorial" title="Lincoln Memorial">
       Lincoln Memorial
      </a>
      ; and the
      <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_Veterans_Memorial" title="Vietnam Veterans Memorial">
       Vietnam Veterans Memorial
      </a>
      . The neoclassical, Georgian, gothic, and modern architectural styles are all reflected among those six structures and many other prominent edifices in Washington. Notable exceptions include buildings constructed in the French
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Second_Empire_(architecture)" title="Second Empire (architecture)">
       Second Empire
      </a>
      style such as the
      <a href="/wiki/Eisenhower_Executive_Office_Building" title="Eisenhower Executive Office Building">
       Eisenhower Executive Office Building
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nps_86-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-nps-86">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Outside downtown Washington, architectural styles are even more varied. Historic buildings are designed primarily in the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Queen_Anne_Style_architecture" title="Queen Anne Style architecture">
       Queen Anne
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Ch%C3%A2teauesque" title="Châteauesque">
       Châteauesque
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Richardsonian_Romanesque" title="Richardsonian Romanesque">
       Richardsonian Romanesque
      </a>
      , Georgian revival,
      <a href="/wiki/Beaux-Arts_architecture" title="Beaux-Arts architecture">
       Beaux-Arts
      </a>
      , and a variety of
      <a href="/wiki/Victorian_architecture" title="Victorian architecture">
       Victorian
      </a>
      styles. Rowhouses are especially prominent in areas developed after the Civil War and typically follow
      <a href="/wiki/Federal_architecture" title="Federal architecture">
       Federalist
      </a>
      and late Victorian designs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        [83]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Georgetown's
      <a href="/wiki/Old_Stone_House_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Old Stone House (Washington, D.C.)">
       Old Stone House
      </a>
      was built in 1765, making it the oldest-standing original building in the city.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        [84]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Founded in 1789,
      <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_University" title="Georgetown University">
       Georgetown University
      </a>
      features a mix of Romanesque and
      <a href="/wiki/Gothic_Revival_architecture" title="Gothic Revival architecture">
       Gothic Revival architecture
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nps_86-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-nps-86">
        [82]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan_Building" title="Ronald Reagan Building">
       Ronald Reagan Building
      </a>
      is the largest building in the District with a total area of approximately 3.1 million square feet (288,000 m
      <sup>
       2
      </sup>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89">
       <a href="#cite_note-89">
        [85]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
