     </table>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_home_rule" title="District of Columbia home rule">
       District of Columbia home rule
      </a>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_mayors_of_Washington,_D.C." title="List of mayors of Washington, D.C.">
       List of mayors of Washington, D.C.
      </a>
      ; and
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_District_of_Columbia_symbols" title="List of District of Columbia symbols">
       List of District of Columbia symbols
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wilsonbldg.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="603" data-file-width="944" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Wilsonbldg.JPG/220px-Wilsonbldg.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Wilsonbldg.JPG/330px-Wilsonbldg.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Wilsonbldg.JPG/440px-Wilsonbldg.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wilsonbldg.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/John_A._Wilson_Building" title="John A. Wilson Building">
         John A. Wilson Building
        </a>
        houses the offices of the mayor and council of the District of Columbia.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Article_One_of_the_United_States_Constitution" title="Article One of the United States Constitution">
       Article One, Section Eight of the United States Constitution
      </a>
      grants the U.S. Congress "exclusive jurisdiction" over the city. The District did not have an elected local government until the passage of the
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_Home_Rule_Act" title="District of Columbia Home Rule Act">
       1973 Home Rule Act
      </a>
      . The Act devolved certain Congressional powers to an
      <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_the_District_of_Columbia" title="Mayor of the District of Columbia">
       elected mayor
      </a>
      , currently
      <a href="/wiki/Muriel_Bowser" title="Muriel Bowser">
       Muriel Bowser
      </a>
      , and the thirteen-member
      <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_District_of_Columbia" title="Council of the District of Columbia">
       Council of the District of Columbia
      </a>
      . However, Congress retains the right to review and overturn laws created by the council and intervene in local affairs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-176">
       <a href="#cite_note-176">
        [170]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Each of the city's eight
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ward_(country_subdivision)" title="Ward (country subdivision)">
       wards
      </a>
      elects a single member of the council and residents elect four at-large members to represent the District as a whole. The council chair is also elected at-large.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177">
       <a href="#cite_note-177">
        [171]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There are 37
      <a href="/wiki/Advisory_Neighborhood_Commission" title="Advisory Neighborhood Commission">
       Advisory Neighborhood Commissions
      </a>
      (ANCs) elected by small neighborhood districts. ANCs can issue recommendations on all issues that affect residents; government agencies take their advice under careful consideration.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178">
       <a href="#cite_note-178">
        [172]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Attorney_General_of_the_District_of_Columbia" title="Attorney General of the District of Columbia">
       Attorney General of the District of Columbia
      </a>
      , currently
      <a href="/wiki/Karl_Racine" title="Karl Racine">
       Karl Racine
      </a>
      , is elected to a four-year term.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179">
       <a href="#cite_note-179">
        [173]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Washington, D.C., observes all
      <a href="/wiki/Federal_holidays_in_the_United_States" title="Federal holidays in the United States">
       federal holidays
      </a>
      and also celebrates
      <a href="/wiki/Emancipation_Day" title="Emancipation Day">
       Emancipation Day
      </a>
      on April 16, which commemorates the end of slavery in the District.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emancipation_26-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-emancipation-26">
        [24]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Washington,_D.C." title="Flag of Washington, D.C.">
       flag of Washington, D.C.
      </a>
      , was adopted in 1938 and is a variation on George Washington's family
      <a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms" title="Coat of arms">
       coat of arms
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180">
       <a href="#cite_note-180">
        [174]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Budgetary_issues">
       Budgetary issues
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      The mayor and council set local taxes and a budget, which must be approved by Congress. The
      <a href="/wiki/Government_Accountability_Office" title="Government Accountability Office">
       Government Accountability Office
      </a>
      and other analysts have estimated that the city's high percentage of tax-exempt property and the Congressional prohibition of commuter taxes create a structural deficit in the District's local budget of anywhere between $470 million and over $1 billion per year. Congress typically provides additional grants for federal programs such as
      <a href="/wiki/Medicaid" title="Medicaid">
       Medicaid
      </a>
      and the operation of the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Capital_Revitalization_and_Self-Government_Improvement_Act_of_1997" title="National Capital Revitalization and Self-Government Improvement Act of 1997">
       local justice system
      </a>
      ; however, analysts claim that the payments do not fully resolve the imbalance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DCAppleseed_181-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DCAppleseed-181">
        [175]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GAO_182-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GAO-182">
        [176]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The city's local government, particularly during the mayoralty of
      <a href="/wiki/Marion_Barry" title="Marion Barry">
       Marion Barry
      </a>
      , was criticized for mismanagement and waste.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183">
       <a href="#cite_note-183">
        [177]
       </a>
      </sup>
      During his administration in 1989,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Washington_Monthly" title="The Washington Monthly">
        The Washington Monthly
       </a>
      </i>
      magazine claimed that the District had "the worst city government in America."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-184">
       <a href="#cite_note-184">
        [178]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 1995, at the start of Barry's fourth term, Congress created the
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_Financial_Control_Board" title="District of Columbia Financial Control Board">
       District of Columbia Financial Control Board
      </a>
      to oversee all municipal spending.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-185">
       <a href="#cite_note-185">
        [179]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Mayor
      <a href="/wiki/Anthony_A._Williams" title="Anthony A. Williams">
       Anthony Williams
      </a>
      won election in 1998 and oversaw a period of
      <a href="/wiki/Urban_renewal" title="Urban renewal">
       urban renewal
      </a>
      and budget surpluses. The District regained control over its finances in 2001 and the oversight board's operations were suspended.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186">
       <a href="#cite_note-186">
        [180]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Voting_rights_debate">
       Voting rights debate
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_voting_rights" title="District of Columbia voting rights">
       District of Columbia voting rights
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Political_party_strength_in_Washington,_D.C." title="Political party strength in Washington, D.C.">
       Political party strength in Washington, D.C.
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:US_Capitol_from_NW.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1944" data-file-width="2592" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/US_Capitol_from_NW.JPG/220px-US_Capitol_from_NW.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/US_Capitol_from_NW.JPG/330px-US_Capitol_from_NW.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/US_Capitol_from_NW.JPG/440px-US_Capitol_from_NW.JPG 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:US_Capitol_from_NW.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/United_States_Congress" title="United States Congress">
         United States Congress
        </a>
        has ultimate authority over the District.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The District is not a state and therefore has no voting representation in the Congress. D.C. residents elect a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Non-voting_members_of_the_United_States_House_of_representatives" title="Non-voting members of the United States House of representatives">
       non-voting delegate
      </a>
      to the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_House_of_Representatives" title="United States House of Representatives">
       House of Representatives
      </a>
      , currently
      <a href="/wiki/Eleanor_Holmes_Norton" title="Eleanor Holmes Norton">
       Eleanor Holmes Norton
      </a>
      (D-
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia%27s_At-large_congressional_district" title="District of Columbia's At-large congressional district">
       D.C. At-Large
      </a>
      ), who may sit on committees, participate in debate, and introduce legislation, but cannot vote on the
      <a href="/wiki/Floor_(legislative)" title="Floor (legislative)">
       House floor
      </a>
      . The District has no official representation in the
      <a href="/wiki/United_States_Senate" title="United States Senate">
       United States Senate
      </a>
      . Neither chamber seats the District's elected
      <a href="/wiki/Shadow_congressperson" title="Shadow congressperson">
       "shadow" representative or senators
      </a>
      . Unlike residents of
      <a href="/wiki/Territories_of_the_United_States" title="Territories of the United States">
       U.S. territories
      </a>
      such as
      <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Rico" title="Puerto Rico">
       Puerto Rico
      </a>
      or
      <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">
       Guam
      </a>
      , which also have non-voting delegates, D.C. residents are subject to all federal taxes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187">
       <a href="#cite_note-187">
        [181]
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the financial year 2012, D.C. residents and businesses paid $20.7 billion in federal taxes; more than the taxes collected from 19 states and the highest
      <a href="/wiki/Federal_tax_revenue_by_state" title="Federal tax revenue by state">
       federal taxes per capita
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188">
       <a href="#cite_note-188">
        [182]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      A 2005 poll found that 78% of Americans did not know that residents of the District of Columbia have less representation in Congress than residents of the 50 states.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-votepoll_189-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-votepoll-189">
        [183]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Efforts to raise awareness about the issue have included campaigns by
      <a href="/wiki/Grassroots" title="Grassroots">
       grassroots
      </a>
      organizations and featuring the city's unofficial motto, "
      <a href="/wiki/No_taxation_without_representation" title="No taxation without representation">
       Taxation Without Representation
      </a>
      ", on
      <a href="/wiki/Vehicle_registration_plates_of_Washington,_D.C." title="Vehicle registration plates of Washington, D.C.">
       D.C. vehicle license plates
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-190">
       <a href="#cite_note-190">
        [184]
       </a>
      </sup>
      There is evidence of nationwide approval for D.C. voting rights; various polls indicate that 61 to 82% of Americans believe that D.C. should have voting representation in Congress.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-votepoll_189-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-votepoll-189">
        [183]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-191">
       <a href="#cite_note-191">
        [185]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Despite public support, attempts to grant the District voting representation, including the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/D.C._statehood_movement" title="D.C. statehood movement">
       D.C. statehood movement
      </a>
      and the proposed
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_Voting_Rights_Amendment" title="District of Columbia Voting Rights Amendment">
       District of Columbia Voting Rights Amendment
      </a>
      , have been unsuccessful.
     </p>
     <p>
      Opponents of D.C. voting rights propose that the
      <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">
       Founding Fathers
      </a>
      never intended for District residents to have a vote in Congress since the Constitution makes clear that representation must come from the states. Those opposed to making D.C. a state claim that such a move would destroy the notion of a separate national capital and that statehood would unfairly grant Senate representation to a single city.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192">
       <a href="#cite_note-192">
        [186]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Sister_cities">
       Sister cities
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      Washington, D.C., has fourteen official
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sister_city" title="Sister city">
       sister city
      </a>
      agreements. Listed in the order each agreement was first established, they are:
      <a href="/wiki/Bangkok" title="Bangkok">
       Bangkok
      </a>
      , Thailand (1962, renewed 2002);
      <a href="/wiki/Dakar" title="Dakar">
       Dakar
      </a>
      , Senegal (1980, renewed 2006);
      <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">
       Beijing
      </a>
      , China (1984, renewed 2004);
      <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">
       Brussels
      </a>
      , Belgium (1985, renewed 2002);
      <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">
       Athens
      </a>
      , Greece (2000);
      <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">
       Paris
      </a>
      , France (2000 as a friendship and cooperation agreement, renewed 2005);
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-193">
       <a href="#cite_note-193">
        [187]
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/City_of_Tshwane_Metropolitan_Municipality" title="City of Tshwane Metropolitan Municipality">
       Pretoria
      </a>
      , South Africa (2002, renewed 2008);
      <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">
       Seoul
      </a>
      , South Korea (2006);
      <a href="/wiki/Accra" title="Accra">
       Accra
      </a>
      , Ghana (2006);
      <a href="/wiki/Sunderland,_Tyne_and_Wear" title="Sunderland, Tyne and Wear">
       Sunderland
      </a>
      , United Kingdom (2006);
      <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">
       Rome
      </a>
      , Italy (2011);
      <a href="/wiki/Ankara" title="Ankara">
       Ankara
      </a>
      , Turkey (2011);
      <a href="/wiki/Bras%C3%ADlia" title="Brasília">
       Brasília
      </a>
      , Brazil (2013); and
      <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">
       Addis Ababa
      </a>
      , Ethiopia (2013).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194">
       <a href="#cite_note-194">
        [188]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Each of the listed cities is a national capital except for Sunderland, which includes the town of
      <a href="/wiki/Washington,_Tyne_and_Wear" title="Washington, Tyne and Wear">
       Washington
      </a>
      , the ancestral home of George Washington's family.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-195">
       <a href="#cite_note-195">
        [189]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Paris and Rome are each formally recognized as a "partner city" due to their special one sister city policy.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-196">
       <a href="#cite_note-196">
        [190]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Education">
       Education
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_colleges_and_universities_in_Washington,_D.C." title="List of colleges and universities in Washington, D.C.">
       List of colleges and universities in Washington, D.C.
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_parochial_and_private_schools_in_Washington,_D.C." title="List of parochial and private schools in Washington, D.C.">
       List of parochial and private schools in Washington, D.C.
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Howard_University_Founders_Library.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2162" data-file-width="3200" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Howard_University_Founders_Library.jpg/220px-Howard_University_Founders_Library.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Howard_University_Founders_Library.jpg/330px-Howard_University_Founders_Library.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Howard_University_Founders_Library.jpg/440px-Howard_University_Founders_Library.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Howard_University_Founders_Library.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Founders Library at
        <a href="/wiki/Howard_University" title="Howard University">
         Howard University
        </a>
        , a
        <a href="/wiki/Historically_black_colleges_and_universities" title="Historically black colleges and universities">
         historically black university
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_Public_Schools" title="District of Columbia Public Schools">
       District of Columbia Public Schools
      </a>
      (DCPS) operates the city's 123 public schools.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-dcps_197-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-dcps-197">
        [191]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The number of students in DCPS steadily decreased for 39 years until 2009. In the 2010–11 school year, 46,191 students were enrolled in the public school system.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DCenroll_198-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DCenroll-198">
        [192]
       </a>
      </sup>
      DCPS has one of the highest-cost yet lowest-performing school systems in the country, both in terms of infrastructure and student achievement.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199">
       <a href="#cite_note-199">
        [193]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Mayor Adrian Fenty's administration made sweeping changes to the system by closing schools, replacing teachers, firing principals, and using private education firms to aid curriculum development.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-200">
       <a href="#cite_note-200">
        [194]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_Public_Charter_School_Board" title="District of Columbia Public Charter School Board">
       District of Columbia Public Charter School Board
      </a>
      monitors the 52 public charter schools in the city.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-201">
       <a href="#cite_note-201">
        [195]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Due to the perceived problems with the traditional public school system, enrollment in public
      <a href="/wiki/Charter_school" title="Charter school">
       charter schools
      </a>
      has steadily increased.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202">
       <a href="#cite_note-202">
        [196]
       </a>
      </sup>
      As of fall 2010, D.C. charter schools had a total enrollment of about 32,000, a 9% increase from the prior year.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DCenroll_198-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-DCenroll-198">
        [192]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The District is also home to 92 private schools, which enrolled approximately 18,000 students in 2008.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203">
       <a href="#cite_note-203">
        [197]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_Public_Library" title="District of Columbia Public Library">
       District of Columbia Public Library
      </a>
      operates 25 neighborhood locations including the landmark
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Luther_King_Jr._Memorial_Library" title="Martin Luther King Jr. Memorial Library">
       Martin Luther King Jr. Memorial Library
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204">
       <a href="#cite_note-204">
        [198]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Private universities include
      <a href="/wiki/American_University" title="American University">
       American University
      </a>
      (AU),
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Catholic_University_of_America" title="The Catholic University of America">
       the Catholic University of America
      </a>
      (CUA),
      <a href="/wiki/Gallaudet_University" title="Gallaudet University">
       Gallaudet University
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_University" title="George Washington University">
       George Washington University
      </a>
      (GW),
      <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_University" title="Georgetown University">
       Georgetown University
      </a>
      (GU),
      <a href="/wiki/Howard_University" title="Howard University">
       Howard University
      </a>
      , and the
      <a href="/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_University" title="Johns Hopkins University">
       Johns Hopkins University
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Paul_H._Nitze_School_of_Advanced_International_Studies" title="Paul H. Nitze School of Advanced International Studies">
       School of Advanced International Studies
      </a>
      (SAIS). The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Corcoran_College_of_Art_and_Design" title="Corcoran College of Art and Design">
       Corcoran College of Art and Design
      </a>
      provides specialized arts instruction and other higher-education institutions offer continuing, distance and adult education. The
      <a href="/wiki/University_of_the_District_of_Columbia" title="University of the District of Columbia">
       University of the District of Columbia
      </a>
      (UDC) is a public university providing undergraduate and graduate education. D.C. residents may also be eligible for a grant of up to $10,000 per year to offset the cost of tuition at any public university in the country.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205">
       <a href="#cite_note-205">
        [199]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The District is known for its medical research institutions such as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Washington_Hospital_Center" title="Washington Hospital Center">
       Washington Hospital Center
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Children%27s_National_Medical_Center" title="Children's National Medical Center">
       Children's National Medical Center
      </a>
      , as well as the
      <a href="/wiki/National_Institutes_of_Health" title="National Institutes of Health">
       National Institutes of Health
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Bethesda,_Maryland" title="Bethesda, Maryland">
       Bethesda, Maryland
      </a>
      . In addition, the city is home to three medical schools and associated teaching hospitals at George Washington, Georgetown, and Howard universities.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206">
       <a href="#cite_note-206">
        [200]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Infrastructure">
       Infrastructure
      </span>
     </h2>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">
       Transportation
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote" role="note">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Transportation_in_Washington,_D.C." title="Transportation in Washington, D.C.">
       Transportation in Washington, D.C.
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metro_Center_upper_level.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="533" data-file-width="800" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Metro_Center_upper_level.jpg/220px-Metro_Center_upper_level.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Metro_Center_upper_level.jpg/330px-Metro_Center_upper_level.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Metro_Center_upper_level.jpg/440px-Metro_Center_upper_level.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Metro_Center_upper_level.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Metro_Center_(Washington_Metro)" title="Metro Center (Washington Metro)">
         Metro Center
        </a>
        is the transfer station for the
        <a href="/wiki/Red_Line_(Washington_Metro)" title="Red Line (Washington Metro)">
         Red
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Orange_Line_(Washington_Metro)" title="Orange Line (Washington Metro)">
         Orange
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/Silver_Line_(Washington_Metro)" title="Silver Line (Washington Metro)">
         Silver
        </a>
        , and
        <a href="/wiki/Blue_Line_(Washington_Metro)" title="Blue Line (Washington Metro)">
         Blue
        </a>
        <a href="/wiki/Washington_Metro" title="Washington Metro">
         Metrorail
        </a>
        lines.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      There are 1,500 miles (2,400 km) of
      <a href="/wiki/Streets_and_highways_of_Washington,_D.C." title="Streets and highways of Washington, D.C.">
       streets, parkways, and avenues
      </a>
      in the District.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-207">
       <a href="#cite_note-207">
        [201]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Due to the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeway_and_expressway_revolts" title="Freeway and expressway revolts">
       freeway revolts
      </a>
      of the 1960s, much of the proposed
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_highway_system" title="Interstate highway system">
       interstate highway system
      </a>
      through the middle of Washington was never built.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_95_in_the_District_of_Columbia" title="Interstate 95 in the District of Columbia">
       Interstate 95
      </a>
      (I-95), the nation's major east coast highway, therefore bends around the District to form the eastern portion of the
      <a href="/wiki/Interstate_495_(Capital_Beltway)" title="Interstate 495 (Capital Beltway)">
       Capital Beltway
      </a>
      . A portion of the proposed highway funding was directed to the region's public transportation infrastructure instead.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208">
       <a href="#cite_note-208">
        [202]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The interstate highways that continue into Washington, including
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_66_in_the_District_of_Columbia" title="Interstate 66 in the District of Columbia">
       I-66
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interstate_395_in_the_District_of_Columbia" title="Interstate 395 in the District of Columbia">
       I-395
      </a>
      , both terminate shortly after entering the city.
     </p>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Washington_Metropolitan_Area_Transit_Authority" title="Washington Metropolitan Area Transit Authority">
       Washington Metropolitan Area Transit Authority
      </a>
      (WMATA) operates the
      <a href="/wiki/Washington_Metro" title="Washington Metro">
       Washington Metro
      </a>
      , the city's
      <a href="/wiki/Rapid_transit" title="Rapid transit">
       rapid transit
      </a>
      system, as well as
      <a href="/wiki/Metrobus_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Metrobus (Washington, D.C.)">
       Metrobus
      </a>
      . Both systems serve the District and its suburbs. Metro opened on March 27, 1976 and, as of July 2014
      <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">
       <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Washington,_D.C.&amp;action=edit">
        [update]
       </a>
      </sup>
      , consists of
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Washington_Metro_stations" title="List of Washington Metro stations">
       91 stations
      </a>
      and 117 miles (188 km) of track.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209">
       <a href="#cite_note-209">
        [203]
       </a>
      </sup>
      With an average of about one million trips each weekday, Metro is the
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_rapid_transit_systems_by_ridership" title="List of United States rapid transit systems by ridership">
       second-busiest rapid transit system
      </a>
      in the country. Metrobus serves over 400,000 riders each weekday and is the nation's
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_local_bus_agencies_by_ridership" title="List of United States local bus agencies by ridership">
       sixth-largest bus system
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-210">
       <a href="#cite_note-210">
        [204]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The city also operates its own
      <a href="/wiki/DC_Circulator" title="DC Circulator">
       DC Circulator
      </a>
      bus system, which connects commercial areas within central Washington.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-211">
       <a href="#cite_note-211">
        [205]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Union_Station_and_Christopher_Columbus_Memorial_Fountain.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1685" data-file-width="3254" height="114" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Union_Station_and_Christopher_Columbus_Memorial_Fountain.jpg/220px-Union_Station_and_Christopher_Columbus_Memorial_Fountain.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Union_Station_and_Christopher_Columbus_Memorial_Fountain.jpg/330px-Union_Station_and_Christopher_Columbus_Memorial_Fountain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Union_Station_and_Christopher_Columbus_Memorial_Fountain.jpg/440px-Union_Station_and_Christopher_Columbus_Memorial_Fountain.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Union_Station_and_Christopher_Columbus_Memorial_Fountain.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Union Station (Washington, D.C.)">
         Union Station
        </a>
        is a transportation hub for passengers on Amtrak, commuter rail lines, and the Washington Metro.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Union_Station_(Washington,_D.C.)" title="Union Station (Washington, D.C.)">
       Union Station
      </a>
      is the city's main train station and services approximately 70,000 people each day. It is
      <a href="/wiki/Amtrak" title="Amtrak">
       Amtrak
      </a>
      's second-busiest station with 4.6 million passengers annually and is the southern terminus for the
      <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">
       Northeast Corridor
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Acela_Express" title="Acela Express">
       Acela Express
      </a>
      routes. Maryland's
      <a href="/wiki/MARC_Train" title="MARC Train">
       MARC
      </a>
      and Virginia's
      <a href="/wiki/Virginia_Railway_Express" title="Virginia Railway Express">
       VRE
      </a>
      commuter trains and the Metrorail
      <a href="/wiki/Red_Line_(Washington_Metro)" title="Red Line (Washington Metro)">
       Red Line
      </a>
      also provide service into Union Station.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212">
       <a href="#cite_note-212">
        [206]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Following renovations in 2011, Union Station became Washington's primary
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intercity_bus" title="Intercity bus">
       intercity bus
      </a>
      transit center.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213">
       <a href="#cite_note-213">
        [207]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Three major airports serve the District.
      <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan_Washington_National_Airport" title="Ronald Reagan Washington National Airport">
       Ronald Reagan Washington National Airport
      </a>
      is across the Potomac River from downtown Washington in
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arlington,_Virginia" title="Arlington, Virginia">
       Arlington, Virginia
      </a>
      and primarily handles domestic flights. Major international flights arrive and depart from
      <a href="/wiki/Washington_Dulles_International_Airport" title="Washington Dulles International Airport">
       Washington Dulles International Airport
      </a>
      , 26.3 miles (42.3 km) west of the District in
      <a href="/wiki/Fairfax_County,_Virginia" title="Fairfax County, Virginia">
       Fairfax
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Loudoun_County,_Virginia" title="Loudoun County, Virginia">
       Loudoun
      </a>
      counties in Virginia.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baltimore-Washington_International_Thurgood_Marshall_Airport" title="Baltimore-Washington International Thurgood Marshall Airport">
       Baltimore-Washington International Thurgood Marshall Airport
      </a>
      is 31.7 miles (51.0 km) northeast of the District in
      <a href="/wiki/Anne_Arundel_County,_Maryland" title="Anne Arundel County, Maryland">
       Anne Arundel County, Maryland
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      According to a 2010 study, Washington-area commuters spent 70 hours a year in traffic delays, which tied with Chicago for having the nation's worst road congestion.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214">
       <a href="#cite_note-214">
        [208]
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, 37% of Washington-area commuters take public transportation to work, the second-highest rate in the country.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215">
       <a href="#cite_note-215">
        [209]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An additional 12% of D.C. commuters walked to work, 6% carpooled, and 3% traveled by bicycle in 2010.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216">
       <a href="#cite_note-216">
        [210]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A 2011 study by
      <a href="/wiki/Walk_Score" title="Walk Score">
       Walk Score
      </a>
      found that Washington was the seventh-most walkable city in the country with 80% of residents living in neighborhoods that are not car dependent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-217">
       <a href="#cite_note-217">
        [211]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      An expected 32% increase in transit usage within the District by 2030 has spurred construction of a new
      <a href="/wiki/DC_Streetcar" title="DC Streetcar">
       DC Streetcar
      </a>
      system to interconnect the city's neighborhoods.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218">
       <a href="#cite_note-218">
        [212]
       </a>
      </sup>
      Construction has also started on an additional
      <a href="/wiki/Silver_Line_(Washington_Metro)" title="Silver Line (Washington Metro)">
       Metro line
      </a>
      that will connect Washington to Dulles airport.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-219">
       <a href="#cite_note-219">
        [213]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The District is part of the regional
      <a href="/wiki/Capital_Bikeshare" title="Capital Bikeshare">
       Capital Bikeshare
      </a>
      program. Started in 2010, it is currently one of the largest
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bicycle_sharing_system" title="Bicycle sharing system">
       bicycle sharing systems
      </a>
      in the country with over 4,351 bicycles and more than 395 stations
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-220">
       <a href="#cite_note-220">
        [214]
       </a>
      </sup>
      all provided by
      <a href="/wiki/PBSC_Urban_Solutions" title="PBSC Urban Solutions">
       PBSC Urban Solutions
      </a>
      . The city is expanding a network of marked bicycle lanes which currently exist on 56 miles (90 km) of streets.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-221">
       <a href="#cite_note-221">
        [215]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Utilities">
       Utilities
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Residuals_processing_facility_-_Dalecarlia_Reservoir_-_Washington_Aqueduct_-_2011.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="714" data-file-width="1165" height="135" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Residuals_processing_facility_-_Dalecarlia_Reservoir_-_Washington_Aqueduct_-_2011.jpg/220px-Residuals_processing_facility_-_Dalecarlia_Reservoir_-_Washington_Aqueduct_-_2011.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Residuals_processing_facility_-_Dalecarlia_Reservoir_-_Washington_Aqueduct_-_2011.jpg/330px-Residuals_processing_facility_-_Dalecarlia_Reservoir_-_Washington_Aqueduct_-_2011.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Residuals_processing_facility_-_Dalecarlia_Reservoir_-_Washington_Aqueduct_-_2011.jpg/440px-Residuals_processing_facility_-_Dalecarlia_Reservoir_-_Washington_Aqueduct_-_2011.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Residuals_processing_facility_-_Dalecarlia_Reservoir_-_Washington_Aqueduct_-_2011.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Processing facilities at
        <a href="/wiki/Dalecarlia_Reservoir" title="Dalecarlia Reservoir">
         Dalecarlia Reservoir
        </a>
        , the city's primary storage basin of drinking water.
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/District_of_Columbia_Water_and_Sewer_Authority" title="District of Columbia Water and Sewer Authority">
       District of Columbia Water and Sewer Authority
      </a>
      (i.e. WASA or D.C. Water) is an independent authority of the D.C. government that provides
      <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">
       drinking water
      </a>
      and wastewater collection in Washington. WASA purchases water from the historic
      <a href="/wiki/Washington_Aqueduct" title="Washington Aqueduct">
       Washington Aqueduct
      </a>
      , which is operated by the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Corps_of_Engineers" title="U.S. Army Corps of Engineers">
       Army Corps of Engineers
      </a>
      . The water, sourced from the Potomac River, is treated and stored in the city's
      <a href="/wiki/Dalecarlia_Reservoir" title="Dalecarlia Reservoir">
       Dalecarlia
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Georgetown_Reservoir" title="Georgetown Reservoir">
       Georgetown
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/McMillan_Reservoir" title="McMillan Reservoir">
       McMillan
      </a>
      reservoirs. The aqueduct provides drinking water for a total of 1.1 million people in the District and Virginia, including Arlington, Falls Church, and a portion of Fairfax County.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-222">
       <a href="#cite_note-222">
        [216]
       </a>
      </sup>
      The authority also provides
      <a href="/wiki/Sewage_treatment" title="Sewage treatment">
       sewage treatment
      </a>
      services for an additional 1.6 million people in four surrounding Maryland and Virginia counties.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-223">
       <a href="#cite_note-223">
        [217]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Pepco" title="Pepco">
       Pepco
      </a>
      is the city's electric utility and services 793,000 customers in the District and suburban Maryland.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-224">
       <a href="#cite_note-224">
        [218]
       </a>
      </sup>
      An 1889 law prohibits overhead wires within much of the historic City of Washington. As a result, all power lines and telecommunication cables are located underground in downtown Washington, and traffic signals are placed at the edge of the street.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-225">
       <a href="#cite_note-225">
        [219]
       </a>
      </sup>
      A plan announced in 2013 would bury an additional 60 miles (97 km) of primary power lines throughout the District.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-226">
       <a href="#cite_note-226">
        [220]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Washington_Gas" title="Washington Gas">
       Washington Gas
      </a>
      is the city's
      <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">
       natural gas
      </a>
      utility and serves over one million customers in the District and its suburbs. Incorporated by Congress in 1848, the company installed the city's first gas lights in the Capitol,the White House, and along Pennsylvania Avenue.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-227">
       <a href="#cite_note-227">
        [221]
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div style="clear:both;">
     </div>

     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">
       Notes
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
      <ol class="references">
       <li id="cite_note-8">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-8">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         By 1790, the Southern states had largely repaid their overseas debts from the Revolutionary War. The Northern states had not, and wanted the federal government to take over their outstanding liabilities. Southern Congressmen agreed to the plan in return for establishing the new national capital at their preferred site on the Potomac River.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crew124_7-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-Crew124-7">
           [7]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-10">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-10">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The Residence Act allowed the President to select a location within Maryland as far east as the Anacostia River. However, Washington shifted the federal territory's borders to the southeast in order to include the city of Alexandria at the District's southern tip. In 1791, Congress amended the Residence Act to approve the new site, including territory ceded by Virginia.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Crew89_9-1">
          <a href="#cite_note-Crew89-9">
           [8]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-71">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-71">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Mean monthly maxima and minima (i.e. the expected highest and lowest temperature readings at any point during the year or given month) calculated based on data at said location from 1981 to 2010.
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-73">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-73">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Official records for Washington, D.C. were kept at 24th and M Streets
         <a href="/wiki/Northwest,_Washington,_D.C." title="Northwest, Washington, D.C.">
          NW
         </a>
         from January 1871 to June 1945, and at Reagan National since July 1945.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
          <a href="#cite_note-72">
           [69]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-93">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-93">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         Until 1890, the Census Bureau counted the City of Washington, Georgetown, and unincorporated portions of Washington County as three separate areas. The data provided in this article from before 1890 are calculated as if the District of Columbia were a single municipality as it is today. Population data for each city prior to 1890 are available.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92">
          <a href="#cite_note-92">
           [88]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-111">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-111">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         These figures count adherents, meaning all full members, their children, and others who regularly attend services. In all of the District, 55% of the population is adherent to any particular religion.
        </span>
       </li>
      </ol>
     </div>

            </tr>
            <tr>
             <td class="navbox-list navbox-even hlist" colspan="2" style="width:100%;padding:0px">
              <div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
               The District of Columbia itself, and
               <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Independent_city_(Virginia)" title="Independent city (Virginia)">
                Virginia's incorporated cities
               </a>
               , are
               <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/County-equivalent" title="County-equivalent">
                county equivalents
               </a>
               . Virginia's incorporated cities are listed under their surrounding county. The incorporated cities bordering more than one county (
               <a href="/wiki/Alexandria,_Virginia" title="Alexandria, Virginia">
                Alexandria
               </a>
               ,
               <a href="/wiki/Falls_Church,_Virginia" title="Falls Church, Virginia">
                Falls Church
               </a>
               and
               <a href="/wiki/Fredericksburg,_Virginia" title="Fredericksburg, Virginia">
                Fredericksburg
               </a>
               ) are listed under the county they were part of before incorporation as a city.
              </div>
             </td>
